<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mesoamerica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Magliabechiano_(folio_77r).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="1270" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28folio_77r%29.jpg/220px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28folio_77r%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28folio_77r%29.jpg/330px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28folio_77r%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28folio_77r%29.jpg/440px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28folio_77r%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Magliabechiano_(folio_77r).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Codex_Magliabechiano" title="Codex Magliabechiano">Codex Magliabechiano</a> from the Loubat collection, 1904</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> chronicles describe the bathing habits of the peoples of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> during and after the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Aztec_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Aztec Empire">conquest</a>.
<a href="/wiki/Bernal_D%C3%ADaz_del_Castillo" title="Bernal Díaz del Castillo">Bernal Díaz del Castillo</a> describes <a href="/wiki/Moctezuma_II" title="Moctezuma II">Moctezuma</a> (the Mexica, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">emperor</a> at the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Cortés</a>) in his <i>Historia verdadera de la conquista de la Nueva España</i> as being "...Very neat and cleanly, bathing every day each afternoon...".
Bathing was not restricted to the elite, but was practised by all people; the chronicler Tomás López Medel wrote after a journey to <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> that "Bathing and the custom of washing oneself is so quotidian (common) amongst the Indians, both of cold and hot lands, as is eating, and this is done in fountains and rivers and other water to which they have access, without anything other than pure water..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mesoamerican bath, known as <i>temazcal</i> in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, from the Nahuatl word <i>temazcalli</i>, a compound of <i>temaz</i> ("steam") and <i>calli</i> ("house"), consists of a room, often in the form of a small dome, with an exterior firebox known as <i>texictle</i> (teʃict͜ɬe) that heats a small portion of the room's wall made of volcanic rocks; after this wall has been heated, water is poured on it to produce steam, an action known as <i>tlasas</i>.  As the steam accumulates in the upper part of the room a person in charge uses a bough to direct the steam to the bathers who are lying on the ground, with which he later gives them a massage, then the bathers scrub themselves with a small flat river stone and finally the person in charge introduces buckets with water with soap and grass used to rinse. This bath had also ritual importance, and was vinculated to the goddess <a href="/wiki/Toci" title="Toci">Toci</a>; it is also therapeutic when medicinal herbs are used in the water for the <i>tlasas</i>. It is still used in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hygiene_in_Christianity#History" title="Hygiene in Christianity">Hygiene in Christianity § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa,_England_-_July_2006.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of the Baths showing a rectangular area of greenish water surrounded by yellow stone buildings with pillars. In the background is the tower of the abbey." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2296" data-file-width="2638" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg/220px-Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg/330px-Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg/440px-Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa,_England_-_July_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Roman_Baths_(Bath)" title="Roman Baths (Bath)">Roman public baths</a> (<a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">Thermae</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>, England.</div></div></div>
<p>Regular bathing was a hallmark of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Elaborate <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">baths</a> were constructed in urban areas to serve the public, who typically demanded the infrastructure to maintain personal cleanliness. The complexes usually consisted of large, swimming pool-like baths, smaller cold and hot pools, saunas, and spa-like facilities where individuals could be depilated, oiled, and massaged. Water was constantly changed by an <a href="/wiki/Roman_aqueduct" title="Roman aqueduct">aqueduct</a>-fed flow. Bathing outside of urban centers involved smaller, less elaborate bathing facilities, or simply the use of clean bodies of water. Roman cities also had large <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer" title="Sanitary sewer">sewers</a>, such as Rome's <a href="/wiki/Cloaca_Maxima" title="Cloaca Maxima">Cloaca Maxima</a>, into which public and private latrines drained. Romans did not have demand-flush toilets but did have some toilets with a continuous flow of water under them. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scent" title="Scent">scented</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oils" title="Oils">oils</a> (mostly from Egypt), among other alternatives.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> places an <a href="/wiki/Ablution_in_Christianity" title="Ablution in Christianity">emphasis on hygiene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squatriti_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Squatriti-79">[79]</a></sup> Despite the denunciation of the <a href="/wiki/Mixed_bathing" title="Mixed bathing">mixed bathing</a> style of Roman pools by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christian" title="Early Christian">early Christian</a> clergy, as well as the pagan custom of women naked bathing in front of men, this did not stop the Church from urging its followers to go to public baths for bathing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squatriti_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Squatriti-79">[79]</a></sup> which contributed to hygiene and good health according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Father" title="Church Father">Church Fathers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mary_Thurlkill_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mary_Thurlkill-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paolo_Squatriti_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paolo_Squatriti-81">[81]</a></sup> The Church built <a href="/wiki/Public_bathing" title="Public bathing">public bathing</a> facilities that were separate for both sexes near <a href="/wiki/Christian_monasticism" title="Christian monasticism">monasteries</a> and pilgrimage sites; also, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popes" title="Popes">popes</a> situated baths within church <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilicas</a> and monasteries since the early Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mary_Thurlkill_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mary_Thurlkill-80">[80]</a></sup> Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregory_the_Great" title="Gregory the Great">Gregory the Great</a> urged his followers on value of <a href="/wiki/Bathing" title="Bathing">bathing</a> as a bodily need.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paolo_Squatriti_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paolo_Squatriti-81">[81]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anal_hygiene" title="Anal hygiene">use of water</a> in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_countries" title="Christian countries">Christian countries</a> is due in part to the Biblical toilet etiquette which encourages washing after all instances of defecation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bidet" title="Bidet">Bidet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bidet_shower" title="Bidet shower">bidet showers</a> were used in regions where water was considered essential for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anal_cleansing" title="Anal cleansing">anal cleansing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contrary to popular belief,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and although some of the Early Christian leaders, such as Boniface I,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> condemned bathing as unspiritual,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bathing" title="Bathing">bathing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> were not lost in Europe with the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Public_bathing" title="Public bathing">Public bathhouses</a> were common in medieval <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> larger towns and cities such as <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regensburg" title="Regensburg">Regensburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Great bathhouses were built in <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine centers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKourkoutidou-NikolaidouTourta199787_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKourkoutidou-NikolaidouTourta199787-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern Europeans were not in the habit of bathing: in the ninth century, <a href="/wiki/Notker_the_Stammerer" title="Notker the Stammerer">Notker the Stammerer</a>, a Frankish monk of St Gall, related a disapproving anecdote that attributed ill results of personal hygiene to an Italian fashion:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There was a certain deacon who followed the habits of the Italians in that he was perpetually trying to resist nature. He used to take baths, he had his head very closely shaved, he polished his skin, he cleaned his nail, he had his hair cut as short as if it were turned on a lathe, and he wore linen underclothes and a snow-white shirt.</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albrecht_Durer,_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1853" data-file-width="1825" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Albrecht_Durer%2C_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg/220px-Albrecht_Durer%2C_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Albrecht_Durer%2C_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg/330px-Albrecht_Durer%2C_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Albrecht_Durer%2C_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg/440px-Albrecht_Durer%2C_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albrecht_Durer,_%22Woman%27s_Bath%22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Woman's Bath</i>, 1496, by <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2822" data-file-width="3762" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg/220px-%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg/330px-%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg/440px-%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%CE%92%CF%85%CE%B6%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C_%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%84%CF%81%CF%8C_%CE%86%CE%BD%CF%89_%CE%A0%CF%8C%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%82_1788.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Bath_(Thessaloniki)" title="Byzantine Bath (Thessaloniki)">Byzantine Bath</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Secular medieval texts constantly refer to the washing of hands before and after meals, but Sone de Nansay, a hero of a 13th-century romance, discovers to his chagrin that the Norwegians do not wash up after eating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In the 11th and 12th centuries, bathing was essential to the Western European upper class: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluniac" title="Cluniac">Cluniac</a> monasteries to which they resorted or retired were always provided with bathhouses, and even the monks were required to take full immersion baths twice a year, at the two Christian festivals of renewal, though exhorted not to uncover themselves from under their bathing sheets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In the 14th century Tuscany, the newlywed couple's bath together was such a firm convention one such couple, in a large coopered tub, is illustrated in fresco in the town hall of San Gimignano.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Catholic religious orders of the <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinians</a>' and <a href="/wiki/Benedictines" title="Benedictines">Benedictines</a>' rules contained <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and inspired by <a href="/wiki/Benedict_of_Nursia" title="Benedict of Nursia">Benedict of Nursia</a> encouragement for the practice of therapeutic bathing; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedictine" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> monks played a role in the development and promotion of <a href="/wiki/Spa" title="Spa">spas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASpiritualHistory_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASpiritualHistory-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bathing had fallen out of fashion in Northern Europe long before the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, when the communal public baths of German cities were in their turn a wonder to Italian visitors. Bathing was replaced by the heavy use of sweat-bathing and <a href="/wiki/Perfume" title="Perfume">perfume</a>, as it was thought in Europe that water could carry disease into the body through the skin. Bathing encouraged an erotic atmosphere that was played upon by the writers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" title="Romance (heroic literature)">romances</a> intended for the upper class;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> in the tale of <a href="/wiki/Melusine" title="Melusine">Melusine</a> the bath was a crucial element of the plot. "Bathing and grooming were regarded with suspicion by moralists, however, because they unveiled the attractiveness of the body. Bathing was said to be a prelude to sin, and in the penitential of <a href="/wiki/Burchard_of_Worms" title="Burchard of Worms">Burchard of Worms</a> we find a full catalogue of the sins that ensued when men and women bathed together."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braunstein_1988:525_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braunstein_1988:525-99">[99]</a></sup> Medieval church authorities believed that public bathing created an environment open to immorality and disease; the 26 public baths of Paris in the late 13th century were strictly overseen by the civil authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braunstein_1988:525_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Braunstein_1988:525-99">[99]</a></sup> At a later date <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> officials even banned public bathing in an unsuccessful effort to halt <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a> epidemics from sweeping Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> also played a prominent role in the development of the British <a href="/wiki/Spa" title="Spa">spas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASpiritualHistory_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-ASpiritualHistory-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the late 19th century, only the elite in Western cities typically possessed indoor facilities for relieving bodily functions. The poorer majority used communal facilities built above <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cesspool" title="Cesspool">cesspools</a> in backyards and courtyards. This changed after Dr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Snow_(physician)" title="John Snow (physician)">John Snow</a> discovered that <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> was <a href="/wiki/Fecal%E2%80%93oral_route" title="Fecal–oral route">transmitted by the fecal contamination</a> of water. Though it took decades for his findings to gain wide acceptance, governments and sanitary reformers were eventually convinced of the health benefits of using <a href="/wiki/Sewerage" title="Sewerage">sewers</a> to keep human waste from contaminating the water. This encouraged the widespread adoption of both the <a href="/wiki/Flush_toilet" title="Flush toilet">flush toilet</a> and the moral imperative that bathrooms should be indoors and as private as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern sanitation was not widely adopted until the 19th and 20th centuries. According to medieval historian Lynn Thorndike, people in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Medieval Europe</a> probably bathed more than people did in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Some time after <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a>'s experiments proved the <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a> and others put them into practice in <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>, hygienic practices came to be regarded as synonymous with <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a>, as they are in modern times.
</p><p>The importance of hand washing for human health – particularly for people in vulnerable circumstances like mothers who had just given birth or wounded soldiers in hospitals – was first recognized in the mid 19th century by two pioneers of hand hygiene: the Hungarian physician <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Semmelweis" title="Ignaz Semmelweis">Ignaz Semmelweis</a> who worked in Vienna, Austria and <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a>, the English "founder of modern nursing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GPPH_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-GPPH-103">[103]</a></sup> At that time most people still believed that infections were caused by foul odors called <a href="/wiki/Miasma_theory" title="Miasma theory">miasmas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam,_Isfahan,_Iran.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9257" data-file-width="10428" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam%2C_Isfahan%2C_Iran.jpg/220px-Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam%2C_Isfahan%2C_Iran.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam%2C_Isfahan%2C_Iran.jpg/330px-Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam%2C_Isfahan%2C_Iran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam%2C_Isfahan%2C_Iran.jpg/440px-Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam%2C_Isfahan%2C_Iran.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam,_Isfahan,_Iran.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ali_Gholi_Agha_hammam" title="Ali Gholi Agha hammam">Ali Gholi Agha hammam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>, Iran.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> stresses the importance of cleanliness and personal hygiene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_hygienical_jurisprudence" title="Islamic hygienical jurisprudence">Islamic hygienical jurisprudence</a>, which dates back to the 7th century, has a number of elaborate rules. <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taharah" title="Taharah">Taharah</a></i></span> (ritual purity) involves performing <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Wudu" title="Wudu">wudu</a></i></span> (ablution) for the five daily <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">salah</a></i></span> (prayers), as well as regularly performing <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ghusl" title="Ghusl">ghusl</a></i></span> (bathing), which led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_bath" title="Turkish bath">bathhouses</a> being built across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islamic_toilet_etiquette" title="Islamic toilet etiquette">Islamic toilet hygiene</a> also requires <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anal_cleansing" title="Anal cleansing">washing with water</a> after using the toilet, for purity and to minimize pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> (8th–13th centuries), its capital city of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> (Iraq) had 65,000 baths, along with a sewer system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Cities and towns of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Islamic_world" title="Medieval Islamic world">medieval Islamic world</a> had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_supply_system" title="Water supply system">water supply systems</a> powered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> technology that supplied <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a> along with much greater quantities of water for ritual washing, mainly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosques" title="Mosques">mosques</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_bath" title="Turkish bath">hammams</a> (baths). Bathing establishments in various cities were rated by Arabic writers in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travel_guide" title="Travel guide">travel guides</a>. Medieval Islamic cities such as Baghdad, <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Spain" title="Islamic Spain">Islamic Spain</a>), <a href="/wiki/Fez,_Morocco" title="Fez, Morocco">Fez</a> (Morocco) and <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> (Egypt) also had sophisticated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waste_disposal" title="Waste disposal">waste disposal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sewage_system" title="Sewage system">sewage systems</a> with interconnected networks of sewers. The city of Fustat also had multi-storey <a href="/wiki/Tenement" title="Tenement">tenement</a> buildings (with up to six floors) with <a href="/wiki/Flush_toilet" title="Flush toilet">flush toilets</a>, which were connected to a water supply system, and <a href="/wiki/Flue" title="Flue">flues</a> on each floor carrying waste to underground channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>A basic form of <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">contagion</a> theory dates back to the Persian medicine in the medieval, where it was proposed by Persian physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Sina" title="Ibn Sina">Ibn Sina</a> (also known as Avicenna) in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i> (1025), the most authoritative medical textbook of the Middle Ages. He mentioned that people can transmit disease to others by breath, noted contagion with <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, and discussed the transmission of disease through water and dirt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The concept of invisible contagion was eventually widely accepted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_scholars" title="Islamic scholars">Islamic scholars</a>. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubid_Sultanate" title="Ayyubid Sultanate">Ayyubid Sultanate</a>, they referred to them as <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Najasat" title="Najasat">najasat</a></i></span> ("impure substances"). The <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a></i></span> scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haj_al-Abdari" title="Ibn al-Haj al-Abdari">Ibn al-Haj al-Abdari</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1250–1336</span>), while discussing <a href="/wiki/Islamic_dietary_laws" title="Islamic dietary laws">Islamic diet</a> and hygiene, gave advice and warnings about how contagion can contaminate water, food, and garments, and could spread through the water supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 9th century, <a href="/wiki/Ziryab" title="Ziryab">Ziryab</a> invented a type of <a href="/wiki/Deodorant" title="Deodorant">deodorant</a> to get rid of bad odors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Menocal_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Menocal-112">[112]</a></sup> He also promoted morning and evening baths, and emphasized the maintenance of personal hygiene. Ziryab is thought to have invented a type of <a href="/wiki/Toothpaste" title="Toothpaste">toothpaste</a>, which he popularized throughout <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic Iberia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sertima_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sertima-113">[113]</a></sup> The exact ingredients of this toothpaste are not currently known,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lebling_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lebling-114">[114]</a></sup> but it was reported to have been both "functional and pleasant to taste."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sertima_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sertima-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soap_and_soap_makers">Soap and soap makers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Soap and soap makers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soap#History" title="Soap">Soap § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marseiller_Seife.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2855" data-file-width="4896" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Marseiller_Seife.jpg/220px-Marseiller_Seife.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Marseiller_Seife.jpg/330px-Marseiller_Seife.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Marseiller_Seife.jpg/440px-Marseiller_Seife.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marseiller_Seife.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marseille_soap" title="Marseille soap">Marseille soap</a> in blocks of 600 g (21 oz)</div></div></div>
<p>Hard toilet <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soap</a> with a pleasant smell was invented in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> when soap-making became an established industry. Recipes for soap-making are described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 865–925</span>), who also gave a recipe for producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerine" title="Glycerine">glycerine</a> from <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a>. In the Middle East, soap was produced from the interaction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatty_oil" title="Fatty oil">fatty oils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> with <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, soap was produced using olive oil together with alkali and <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a>. Soap was exported from Syria to other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> and to Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Two key Islamic innovations in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soapmaking" title="Soapmaking">soapmaking</a> was the invention of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bar_soap" title="Bar soap">bar soap</a>, described by al-Razi, and the addition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scent" title="Scent">scents</a> using <a href="/wiki/Perfume" title="Perfume">perfume</a> technology perfected in the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 15th century, the manufacture of soap in the Christendom had become virtually industrialized, with sources in <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castile_(historical_region)" title="Castile (historical region)">Castile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In the 17th century the Spanish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholic</a> manufacturers purchased the <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a> on <a href="/wiki/Castile_soap" title="Castile soap">Castile soap</a> from the cash-strapped <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Carolinian</a> government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Industrially-manufactured bar soaps became available in the late 18th century, as advertising campaigns in Europe and America promoted popular awareness of the relationship between cleanliness and health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major contribution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_missionaries" title="Christian missionaries">Christian missionaries</a> in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and other places was better <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> of the people through hygiene and introducing and distributing the <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soap</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and "cleanliness and hygiene became an important marker of being identified as a Christian".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_hygienic_customs">Religious hygienic customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Religious hygienic customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">Ritual purification</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ablution_in_Christianity" title="Ablution in Christianity">Ablution in Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hygiene_in_Christianity" title="Hygiene in Christianity">Hygiene in Christianity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_hygienical_jurisprudence" title="Islamic hygienical jurisprudence">Islamic hygienical jurisprudence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ritual_washing_in_Judaism" title="Ritual washing in Judaism">Ritual washing in Judaism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_toilet_etiquette" title="Islamic toilet etiquette">Islamic toilet etiquette</a></div>
<p>Many religions require or encourage ritual purification via bathing or immersing the hands in water. In <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, washing oneself via <i><a href="/wiki/Wudu" title="Wudu">wudu</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Ghusl" title="Ghusl">ghusl</a></i> is necessary for performing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">prayer</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Islamic tradition</a> also lists a variety of rules concerning proper hygiene after using the bathroom. The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> mandates the washing of the hands and face prior to the <a href="/wiki/Obligatory_Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_prayers" title="Obligatory Baháʼí prayers">obligatory Baháʼí prayers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a> requires a <i><a href="/wiki/Mikveh" title="Mikveh">mikveh</a></i> bath following menstruation and childbirth, while <a href="/wiki/Handwashing_in_Judaism" title="Handwashing in Judaism">washing the hands</a> is performed upon waking up and before eating bread. <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">Water</a> plays a role in Christian rituals as well,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Z._Wahrman_2016_46–48_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Z._Wahrman_2016_46–48-129">[129]</a></sup> and in certain <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denominations of Christianity</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a> prescribes several kinds of <a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">hand washing</a> for example after leaving the latrine, lavatory or bathhouse, or before prayer, or after eating a meal, or ritual handwashing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Z._Wahrman_2016_46–48_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Z._Wahrman_2016_46–48-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>First attested in English in 1676, the word <i>hygiene</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>hygiène</i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latinisation_(literature)" title="Latinisation (literature)">latinisation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> ὑγιεινή (τέχνη) <i>hygieinē technē</i>, meaning "(art) of health", from ὑγιεινός <i>hygieinos</i>, "good for the health, healthy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> in turn from ὑγιής (<i>hygiēs</i>), "healthful, sound, salutary, wholesome".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Greece" title="Religion in ancient Greece">ancient Greek religion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygeia" title="Hygeia">Hygeia</a> (Ὑγίεια) was the personification of health, cleanliness, and hygiene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hygiene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: External links">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin: 0 0 0.8em 0.8em; width: 18.55em; border-collapse: collapse; font-size: 87%; clear: right; line-height:1.3em; background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;float:right;"><div style="float:left;"><a href="https://meta.wikimedia.org/wiki/Wiki_Project_Med/App" title="meta:Wiki Project Med/App"><img alt="The offline app allows you to download all of Wikipedia's medical articles in an app to access them when you have no Internet." data-file-height="47" data-file-width="42" decoding="async" height="47" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Tango_Phone_medapp.svg/42px-Tango_Phone_medapp.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Tango_Phone_medapp.svg/63px-Tango_Phone_medapp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Tango_Phone_medapp.svg/84px-Tango_Phone_medapp.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></a></div><div style="float:left;width:6px;padding:1px;height:47px;"></div>Wikipedia's health care articles can be viewed offline with the <b><a class="extiw" href="https://meta.wikimedia.org/wiki/Wiki_Project_Med/App" title="meta:Wiki Project Med/App">Medical Wikipedia app</a></b>.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/60px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/80px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Look up <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Special:Search/hygiene" title="wiktionary:Special:Search/hygiene">hygiene</a></b></i> in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.</div></div>
